The program allows  <i>walmart.com</i> customers to buy goods online with a free shipping option, and have goods shipped to the nearest store for pickup.<sup id="cite_ref-site-to-store_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-site-to-store-241">[241]</a></sup></p><p>On September 15, 2017, Walmart announced that it would build a new headquarters in Bentonville to replace its current 1971 building and consolidate operations that have spread out to 20 different buildings throughout Bentonville.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Finance_and_governance">Finance and governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Finance and governance" data-section="38" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>For the <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_year" title="Fiscal year">fiscal year</a> ending January 31, 2019, Walmart reported <a href="/wiki/Net_income" title="Net income">net income</a> of <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$6.67<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> on $514.405<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion of revenue. The company's international operations accounted for $120.824<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion, or 23.7 percent, of its $510.329 billion of sales.<sup id="cite_ref-xbrlus_1_3-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xbrlus_1-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Form10K_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Form10K-5">[5]</a></sup> Walmart is the world's 29th-largest public corporation, according to the <a href="/wiki/Forbes_Global_2000" title="Forbes Global 2000">Forbes Global 2000</a> list, and the largest public corporation when ranked by revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-forbes_global_2000_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forbes_global_2000-243">[243]</a></sup></p><p>Walmart is governed by an eleven-member board of directors elected annually by <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a>. <a href="/wiki/Greg_Penner" title="Greg Penner">Gregory B. Penner</a>, son-in-law of <a href="/wiki/S._Robson_Walton" title="S. Robson Walton">S. Robson Walton</a> and the grandson-in-law of Sam Walton, serves as <a href="/wiki/Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman">chairman</a> of the board. Doug McMillon serves as president and chief executive officer. Current members of the board are:<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Form10K_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Form10K-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sorkin_Rusli_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sorkin_Rusli-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<ul><li>Gregory B. Penner, chairman of the board of directors of Walmart Inc. and general partner of Madrone Capital Partners</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cesar_Conde" title="Cesar Conde">Cesar Conde</a>, chairman of <a href="/wiki/NBCUniversal_International_Networks" title="NBCUniversal International Networks">NBCUniversal International Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/NBCUniversal_Cable_Entertainment_Group#NBCUniversal_Telemundo_Enterprises" title="NBCUniversal Cable Entertainment Group">NBCUniversal Telemundo Enterprises</a></li>
<li>Timothy P. Flynn, retired CEO of <a href="/wiki/KPMG" title="KPMG">KPMG International</a></li>
<li>Sarah Friar, CEO of <a href="/wiki/Nextdoor" title="Nextdoor">Nextdoor</a></li>
<li>Carla A. Harris, Vice chairman of Wealth Management, head of multicultural client strategy, managing director and senior client advisor at <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Stanley" title="Morgan Stanley">Morgan Stanley</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_W._Horton" title="Thomas W. Horton">Tom Horton</a>, senior advisor at <a href="/wiki/Warburg_Pincus" title="Warburg Pincus">Warburg Pincus, LLC</a>, and retired chairman and CEO of <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines" title="American Airlines">American Airlines</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marissa_Mayer" title="Marissa Mayer">Marissa A. Mayer</a>, Co-founder of Lumi Labs, Inc., and former president and CEO of <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!, Inc.</a></li>
<li>Doug McMillon, president and CEO of Walmart</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Steven_Reinemund" title="Steven Reinemund">Steven S. Reinemund</a>, retired dean of business at <a href="/wiki/Wake_Forest_University" title="Wake Forest University">Wake Forest University</a> and retired chairman and CEO of <a href="/wiki/PepsiCo" title="PepsiCo">PepsiCo, Inc.</a></li>
<li>S. Robson "Rob" Walton, retired chairman of the board of directors of Walmart Inc.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Steuart_Walton" title="Steuart Walton">Steuart Walton</a>, founder of RZC Investments, LLC.</li></ul><p>Notable former members of the board include <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> (1985–1992)<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tom_Coughlin_(Walmart_executive)" title="Tom Coughlin (Walmart executive)">Tom Coughlin</a> (2003–2004), the latter having served as vice chairman. Clinton left the board before the <a href="/wiki/1992_United_States_presidential_election" title="1992 United States presidential election">1992 U.S. presidential election</a>, and Coughlin left in December 2005 after pleading guilty to wire fraud and tax evasion for stealing hundreds of thousands of dollars from Walmart.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup></p><p>After Sam Walton's death in 1992, Don Soderquist, Chief Operating Officer and Senior Vice Chairman, became known as the "Keeper of the Culture".<sup id="cite_ref-Soderquist_Donald_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soderquist_Donald-248">[248]</a></sup></p>

<td>11,361
</td></tr></tbody></table><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership">Ownership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Ownership" data-section="39" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Walmart Inc. is a <a href="/wiki/Joint-stock_company" title="Joint-stock company">joint-stock company</a> registered with the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Securities_and_Exchange_Commission" title="U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission">U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission</a>. As of March 2017,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> it has 3,292,377,090 outstanding shares. These are held mainly by the <a href="/wiki/Walton_family" title="Walton family">Walton family</a>, a number of <a href="/wiki/Institutional_investor" title="Institutional investor">institutions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutual_fund" title="Mutual fund">funds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup></p>
<ul><li>43.00% (1,415,891,131): <a href="/wiki/Walton_family" title="Walton family">Walton Enterprises</a> LLC</li>
<li>5.30% (174,563,205): <a href="/wiki/Walton_family" title="Walton family">Walton Family</a> Holdings Trust<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup></li>
<li>3.32% (102,036,399): <a href="/wiki/The_Vanguard_Group" title="The Vanguard Group">The Vanguard Group</a>, Inc</li>
<li>2.37% (72,714,226): <a href="/wiki/State_Street_Corporation" title="State Street Corporation">State Street Corporation</a></li>
<li>1.37% (42,171,892): <a href="/wiki/BlackRock" title="BlackRock">BlackRock</a> Institutional Trust Company</li>
<li>0.94% (28,831,721): <a href="/wiki/The_Vanguard_Group" title="The Vanguard Group">Vanguard</a> Total Stock Market Index Fund</li>
<li>0.77% (23,614,578): <a href="/wiki/BlackRock" title="BlackRock">BlackRock</a> Fund Advisors</li>
<li>0.71% (21,769,126): <a href="/wiki/Dodge_%26_Cox" title="Dodge &amp; Cox">Dodge &amp; Cox</a> Inc</li>
<li>0.68% (20,978,727): <a href="/wiki/The_Vanguard_Group" title="The Vanguard Group">Vanguard</a> 500 Index Fund</li>
<li>0.65% (20,125,838): <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America" title="Bank of America">Bank of America</a> Corporation</li>
<li>0.57% (17,571,058): <a href="/wiki/The_Bank_of_New_York_Mellon" title="The Bank of New York Mellon">Bank of New York Mellon</a> Corporation</li>
<li>0.57% (17,556,128): <a href="/wiki/Northern_Trust" title="Northern Trust">Northern Trust</a> Corporation</li>
<li>0.55% (16,818,165): <a href="/wiki/The_Vanguard_Group" title="The Vanguard Group">Vanguard</a> Institutional Index Fund-Institutional Index Fund</li>
<li>0.55% (16,800,850): <a href="/wiki/State_Farm_Insurance" class="mw-redirect" title="State Farm Insurance">State Farm</a> Mutual Automobile Insurance Co</li>
<li>0.52% (15,989,827): <a href="/wiki/SPDR" title="SPDR">SPDR</a> S&amp;P 500 ETF Trust</li></ul><h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Competition" data-section="40" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In North America, Walmart's primary competitors include <a href="/wiki/Grocery_store" title="Grocery store">grocery stores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Department_store" title="Department store">department stores</a> like <a href="/wiki/Aldi" title="Aldi">Aldi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lidl" title="Lidl">Lidl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kmart" title="Kmart">Kmart</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kroger" title="Kroger">Kroger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ingles" title="Ingles">Ingles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Publix" title="Publix">Publix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">Target</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harris_Teeter" title="Harris Teeter">Harris Teeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meijer" title="Meijer">Meijer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winn_Dixie" class="mw-redirect" title="Winn Dixie">Winn Dixie</a>, Canada's The <a href="/wiki/Real_Canadian_Superstore" title="Real Canadian Superstore">Real Canadian Superstore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sobeys" title="Sobeys">Sobeys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giant_Tiger" title="Giant Tiger">Giant Tiger</a>, and Mexico's <a href="/wiki/Comercial_Mexicana" title="Comercial Mexicana">Comercial Mexicana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soriana" title="Soriana">Soriana</a>. Competitors of Walmart's Sam's Club division are <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> and the smaller <a href="/wiki/BJ%27s_Wholesale_Club" title="BJ's Wholesale Club">BJ's Wholesale Club</a> chain. Walmart's move into the grocery business in the late 1990s set it against major supermarket chains in both the United States and Canada. Several smaller retailers, primarily <a href="/wiki/Variety_store" title="Variety store">dollar stores</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Family_Dollar" title="Family Dollar">Family Dollar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dollar_General" title="Dollar General">Dollar General</a>, have been able to find a small niche market and compete successfully against Walmart.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[299]</a></sup> In 2004, Walmart responded by testing its own dollar store concept, a subsection of some stores called "Pennies-n-Cents."<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[300]</a></sup></p><p>Walmart also had to face fierce competition in some foreign markets. For example, in Germany it had captured just 2<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of the German food market following its entry into the market in 1997 and remained "a secondary player" behind <a href="/wiki/Aldi" title="Aldi">Aldi</a> with 19<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Struggling_In_Germany_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Struggling_In_Germany-301">[301]</a></sup> Walmart continues to do well in the UK, where its Asda subsidiary is the second-largest retailer.<sup id="cite_ref-A_Bumpy_Ride_in_Europe_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_Bumpy_Ride_in_Europe-302">[302]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2006, after entering the South Korean market in 1998, Walmart sold all 16<span class="nowrap"> </span>of its South Korean outlets to <a href="/wiki/Shinsegae" title="Shinsegae">Shinsegae</a>, a local retailer, for <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$882<span class="nowrap"> </span>million</span>. Shinsegae re-branded the Walmarts as <a href="/wiki/E-mart" title="E-mart">E-mart</a> stores.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_wmt_skorea_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_wmt_skorea-303">[303]</a></sup></p><p>Walmart struggled to export its brand elsewhere as it rigidly tried to reproduce its model overseas. In China, Walmart hopes to succeed by adapting and doing things preferable to Chinese citizens. For example, it found that Chinese consumers preferred to select their own live fish and seafood; stores began displaying the meat uncovered and installed fish tanks, leading to higher sales.<sup id="cite_ref-China_reinvent_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-China_reinvent-304">[304]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Customer_base">Customer base</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Customer base" data-section="41" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Walmart customers cite low prices as the most important reason for shopping there.<sup id="cite_ref-nytmorethanprice_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytmorethanprice-305">[305]</a></sup> The average U.S. Walmart customer's income is below the national average.<sup id="cite_ref-walmart2006_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walmart2006-306">[306]</a></sup> A 2006 Walmart report also indicated that Walmart customers are sensitive to higher utility costs and gas prices.<sup id="cite_ref-walmart2006_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walmart2006-306">[306]</a></sup> A poll indicated that after the <a href="/wiki/2004_United_States_presidential_election" title="2004 United States presidential election">2004 US presidential election</a>, 76<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of voters who shopped at Walmart once a week voted for <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> while only 23 percent supported senator <a href="/wiki/John_Kerry" title="John Kerry">John Kerry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-zogbypoll_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zogbypoll-307">[307]</a></sup> When measured against similar retailers in the U.S., frequent Walmart shoppers were rated the most politically <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup> Thus, as of 2014,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> the "majority (54<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent) [of] Americans who prefer shopping at Walmart report that they oppose <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a>, while 40 percent are in favor of it."<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup></p><p>Due to its prominence in the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Belt" title="Bible Belt">Bible Belt</a>, Walmart is known for its "tradition of tailoring its service to churchgoing customers".<sup id="cite_ref-Gilgoff_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilgoff-310">[310]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sellers_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-311">[311]</a></sup> Walmart only carries <a href="/wiki/Censorship_of_music" class="mw-redirect" title="Censorship of music">clean versions</a> of hip-hop <a href="/wiki/Compact_Disc" class="mw-redirect" title="Compact Disc">audio CDs</a> and in cooperation with <a href="/wiki/The_Timothy_Plan" title="The Timothy Plan">The Timothy Plan</a>, places "plastic sheathes over suggestive women's periodicals and banned 'lad mags' such as <a href="/wiki/Maxim_(magazine)" title="Maxim (magazine)">Maxim</a>" magazine.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilgoff_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilgoff-310">[310]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sellers_311-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers-311">[311]</a></sup> In addition, Walmart also caters to its Christian customer base by selling <a href="/wiki/Christian_media" title="Christian media">Christian books and media</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Gilgoff_310-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilgoff-310">[310]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Collidge_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collidge-312">[312]</a></sup> "such as <a href="/wiki/VeggieTales" title="VeggieTales">VeggieTales</a> videos and <a href="/wiki/The_Purpose-Driven_Life" class="mw-redirect" title="The Purpose-Driven Life">The Purpose-Driven Life</a>", which earns the company over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> annually.<sup id="cite_ref-Collidge_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collidge-312">[312]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sellers2_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sellers2-313">[313]</a></sup></p><p>In 2006, Walmart took steps to expand its U.S. customer base, announcing a modification in its U.S. stores from a "one-size-fits-all" merchandising strategy to one designed to "reflect each of six demographic groups—African-Americans, the affluent, empty-nesters, Hispanics, suburbanites, and rural residents."<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_06_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_06-314">[314]</a></sup> Around six months later, it unveiled a new slogan:<i> "Saving people money so they can live better lives"</i>. This reflects the three main groups into which Walmart categorizes its 200<span class="nowrap"> </span>million customers: "brand aspirationals" (people with low incomes who are obsessed with big name brands), "price-sensitive affluents" (wealthier shoppers who love deals), and "value-price shoppers" (people who like low prices and cannot afford much more).<sup id="cite_ref-nytmorethanprice_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytmorethanprice-305">[305]</a></sup> Walmart has also made steps to appeal to more <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberal</a> customers, for example, by rejecting the <a href="/wiki/American_Family_Association" title="American Family Association">American Family Association</a>'s recommendations and carrying the DVD <i><a href="/wiki/Brokeback_Mountain" title="Brokeback Mountain">Brokeback Mountain</a></i>, a love story between two gay cowboys in Wyoming.<sup id="cite_ref-brokeback_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brokeback-315">[315]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sales_of_guns_and_ammunition">Sales of guns and ammunition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Sales of guns and ammunition" data-section="42" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Walmart stopped selling handguns in all U.S. states, except for <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-Bhattarai_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhattarai-316">[316]</a></sup></p><p>In 2018, Walmart stopped selling guns and ammunition to persons younger than 21, following a similar move by <a href="/wiki/Dick%27s_Sporting_Goods" title="Dick's Sporting Goods">Dick's Sporting Goods</a> on the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-Curbs_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curbs-317">[317]</a></sup> In the same year, Walmart stopped selling <a href="/wiki/Assault_weapon" title="Assault weapon">military-style rifles</a> that were commonly used in <a href="/wiki/Mass_shootings_in_the_United_States" title="Mass shootings in the United States">mass shootings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bhattarai_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bhattarai-316">[316]</a></sup></p><p>As of 2019, Walmart was a major retailer of firearms and ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-Corkery_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corkery-318">[318]</a></sup> In 2019, after 22 people were killed in a <a href="/wiki/2019_El_Paso_shooting" title="2019 El Paso shooting">mass shooting at a Walmart store in El Paso, Texas</a>, Walmart announced that it would stop selling all handgun ammunition and certain <a href="/wiki/Short-barreled_rifle" title="Short-barreled rifle">short-barreled rifle</a> ammunition.<sup id="cite_ref-Corkery_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corkery-318">[318]</a></sup> The company also announced that it would stop selling handguns in Alaska, the only state where the company still sold handguns.<sup id="cite_ref-Curbs_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curbs-317">[317]</a></sup> The move was expected to reduce Walmart's U.S. market share in ammunition from around 20% to around 6–9%.<sup id="cite_ref-Curbs_317-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curbs-317">[317]</a></sup> Walmart also stated that was "respectfully requesting" that customers not <a href="/wiki/Open_carry_in_the_United_States" title="Open carry in the United States">openly carry</a> weapons in Walmart stores, except for  authorized law enforcement officers.<sup id="cite_ref-Corkery_318-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Corkery-318">[318]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Curbs_317-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curbs-317">[317]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology">Technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Technology" data-section="43" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Open_source_software">Open source software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Open source software" data-section="44" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>Many Walmart technology projects are coded in the open and available through the Walmart Labs <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">GitHub</a> repository<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source software</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Open_Source_Initiative" title="Open Source Initiative">OSI approved</a> <a href="/wiki/Apache_License" title="Apache License">Apache V2.0 license</a>. As of November 2016,<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> 141 public Github projects are listed.
</p><p>During a migration of the walmart.com retail platform to <a href="/wiki/React_(JavaScript_library)" class="mw-redirect" title="React (JavaScript library)">Facebook React</a> and <a href="/wiki/Node.js" title="Node.js">Node.js</a>, the Electrode<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[320]</a></sup> project was created to power the e-commerce platform which serves 80 million visitors per month and 15 million items.
</p><p>Electrode provides various developer enhancements and tools for the developer including Node.js configuration and feature management.
</p><p>Alex Grigoryan<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup> of Walmart Labs released a statement<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup> on Medium.com on October 3, 2016, explaining the details of the applications and the scale that they operate at Walmart.
</p>
<h4 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_data_analytics">Big data analytics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Big data analytics" data-section="45" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h4>
<p>As the largest retailer in the U.S., Walmart collects and analyzes a large amount of consumer data. The <a href="/wiki/Big_data" title="Big data">big data</a> sets are <a href="/wiki/Data_mining" title="Data mining">mined</a> for use in <a href="/wiki/Predictive_analytics" title="Predictive analytics">predictive analytics</a>, which allow the company to optimize operations by predicting customer's habits. Walmart's <a href="/wiki/Datacenter" class="mw-redirect" title="Datacenter">datacenter</a> is unofficially referred to as <a href="/wiki/Area_71" title="Area 71">Area 71</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2011, Walmart acquired <a href="/wiki/Kosmix" class="mw-redirect" title="Kosmix">Kosmix</a> to develop software for analyzing real-time data streams.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup> In August 2012, Walmart announced its Polaris search engine.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup></p><p>The amount of data gathered by Walmart has raised privacy concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt1_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1-326">[326]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(5)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Charity" data-section="46" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-5 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-5">
<p>Sam Walton believed that the company's contribution to society was the fact that it operated efficiently, thereby lowering the <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_living" title="Cost of living">cost of living</a> for customers, and, therefore, in that sense was a "powerful force for good", despite his refusal to contribute cash to philanthropic causes.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup> Having begun to feel that his wealth attracted people who wanted nothing more than a "handout", he explained that while he believed his family had been fortunate and wished to use his wealth to aid worthy causes like education, they could not be expected to "solve every personal problem that comes to [their] attention". He explained later in his autobiography, "We feel very strongly that Wal-Mart really is <i>not</i>, and <i>should not</i> be, in the charity business," stating "any debit has to be passed along to somebody—either shareholders or our customers."<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup> Since Sam Walton's death in 1992, however, Walmart and the Walmart Foundation dramatically increased charitable giving. For example, in 2005, Walmart donated <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$20<span class="nowrap"> </span>million</span> in cash and merchandise for Hurricane Katrina relief. Today, Walmart's charitable donations approach <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$1<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> each year.<sup id="cite_ref-Charity_Evaluated_331-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charity_Evaluated-331">[331]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(6)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Economic impact" data-section="47" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-6 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-6">
<p>Kenneth Stone, Professor of Economics at Iowa State University, in a paper published in <i>Farm Foundation</i> in 1997, found that some small towns can lose almost half of their retail trade within ten years of a Walmart store opening. He compared the changes to previous competitors small town shops have faced in the past—from the development of the railroads and the Sears Roebuck catalog to shopping malls. He concludes that small towns are more affected by "discount mass merchandiser stores" than larger towns and that shop owners who adapt to the ever-changing retail market can "co-exist and even thrive in this type of environment."<sup id="cite_ref-Rural_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rural-48">[48]</a></sup></p><p>One study found Walmart's entry into a new market has a profound impact on its competition. When a Walmart opens in a new market, median sales drop 40 percent at similar high-volume stores, 17 percent at supermarkets and 6 percent at drugstores, according to a June 2009 study by researchers at several universities and led by the Tuck School of Business at <a href="/wiki/Dartmouth_College" title="Dartmouth College">Dartmouth College</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Loyola_University_Chicago" title="Loyola University Chicago">Loyola University Chicago</a> study suggested that the impact a Walmart store has on a local business is correlated to its distance from that store. The leader of that study admits that this factor is stronger in smaller towns and doesn't apply to more urban areas saying "It'd be so tough to nail down what's up with Wal-Mart".<sup id="cite_ref-wpost_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wpost-333">[333]</a></sup> These findings are underscored by another study conducted in 2009 by the National Bureau of Economics that showed "large, negative effects" for competing businesses within 5 to 10 miles (8 to 16 km) of the newly opening big-box retailer. This same study also found that the local retailers experience virtually no benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup> Walmart's negative effects on local retailers may be partially explained by studies that find that local firms re-invest nearly 63 percent more of profits in other local businesses compared to chain retailers, as found by the Maine Center of Economic Policy in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup></p><p>David Merriman, Joseph Persky, Julie Davis and Ron Baiman did a study in <i><a href="/wiki/Economic_Development_Quarterly" title="Economic Development Quarterly">Economic Development Quarterly</a></i> outlining the impacts of Walmart in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. The study draws from three annual surveys of enterprises within a four-mile radius of a new Chicago Walmart and it "shows that the probability of going out of business was significantly higher for establishments close to that store". The study illustrated how approximately 300 jobs were lost due to the opening of the store, which is about equivalent to Walmart's employment in the area. The overall findings of this study reinforce the "contention that large-city Walmarts, like those in small towns, absorb retail sales from nearby stores without significantly expanding the market" as this is one of the first studies of Walmarts economic impacts on local economies.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup></p>
<ul><li>A 2001 <a href="/wiki/McKinsey_%26_Company" title="McKinsey &amp; Company">McKinsey</a> Global Institute study of U.S. labor productivity growth between 1995 and 2000 concluded that "Wal-Mart directly and indirectly caused the bulk of the productivity acceleration" in the retail sector.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Solow" title="Robert Solow">Robert Solow</a>, a Nobel laureate in economics and an adviser to the study, stated that "[b]y far the most important factor in that [growth] is Wal-Mart."<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Economic_Policy_Institute" title="Economic Policy Institute">Economic Policy Institute</a> estimates that between 2001 and 2006, Wal-Mart's trade deficit with China alone eliminated nearly 200,000 U.S. jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">[339]</a></sup> Another study at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Missouri" title="University of Missouri">University of Missouri</a> found that a new store increases net retail employment in the county by 100 jobs in the short term, half of which disappear over five years as other retail establishments close.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2004 paper by two professors at <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_State_University" title="Pennsylvania State University">Pennsylvania State University</a> found that U.S. counties with Walmart stores suffered increased poverty compared with counties without Wal-Marts.<sup id="cite_ref-PSUstudy_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PSUstudy-341">[341]</a></sup> They hypothesized that this could be due to the displacement of workers from higher-paid jobs in the retailers customers no longer choose to patronize, Wal-Mart providing less local charity than the replaced businesses, or a shrinking pool of local leadership and reduced <a href="/wiki/Social_capital" title="Social capital">social capital</a> due to a reduced number of local independent businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-PSUstudy_341-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PSUstudy-341">[341]</a></sup> Dr <a href="/wiki/Raj_Patel" title="Raj Patel">Raj Patel</a>, author of "<a href="/wiki/Stuffed_and_Starved" title="Stuffed and Starved">Stuffed and Starved</a>: Markets, Power and the Hidden Battle for the World Food System", said in a lecture at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Melbourne" title="University of Melbourne">University of Melbourne</a> on September 18, 2007, that a study in Nebraska looked at two different Wal-Marts, the first of which had just arrived and "was in the process of driving everyone else out of business but, to do that, they cut their prices to the bone, very, very low prices". In the other Wal-Mart, "they had successfully destroyed the local economy, there was a sort of economic crater with Wal-Mart in the middle; and, in that community, the prices were 17<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent higher".<sup id="cite_ref-RNBigIdeas_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNBigIdeas-342">[342]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2005 story in <i>The Washington Post</i> reported that "Wal-Mart's discounting on food alone boosts the welfare of American shoppers by at least <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$50<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> per year."<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup> A study in 2005 at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> (MIT) measured the effect on <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">consumer welfare</a> and found that the poorest segment of the population benefits the most from the existence of discount retailers.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup></li>
<li>A June 2006 article published by the <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Mises_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Ludwig von Mises Institute">Ludwig von Mises Institute</a> suggested that Wal-Mart has a positive impact on small business.<sup id="cite_ref-ultimate_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ultimate-345">[345]</a></sup> It argued that while Wal-Mart's low prices caused some existing businesses to close, the chain also created new opportunities for other small business, and so "the process of <a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">creative destruction</a> unleashed by Wal-Mart has no statistically significant impact on the overall size of the small business sector in the United States."<sup id="cite_ref-sobel_dean_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sobel_dean-346">[346]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2006, American newspaper columnist <a href="/wiki/George_Will" title="George Will">George Will</a> named Wal-Mart "the most prodigious job-creator in the history of the private sector in this galaxy" and that "[b]y lowering consumer prices, Wal-Mart costs about 50 retail jobs among competitors <i>for every 100 jobs Wal-Mart creates</i>". In terms of economic effects, Will states that "Wal-Mart and its effects save shoppers more than <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$200<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> a year, dwarfing such government programs as <a href="/wiki/Supplemental_Nutrition_Assistance_Program" title="Supplemental Nutrition Assistance Program">food stamps</a> (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$28.6<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span>) and the <a href="/wiki/Earned_income_tax_credit" title="Earned income tax credit">earned income tax credit</a> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$34.6<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span>)".<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup></li>
<li>A 2014 story in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> reported that the Wal-Mart Foundation was boosting its efforts to work with U.S. manufacturers. In February 2014, the Walmart Foundation pledged <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$10<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> to support domestic manufacturers and announced plans to buy <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$250<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion</span> worth of American-made products in the next decade.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup></li></ul></section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(7)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_relations">Labor relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Labor relations" data-section="48" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-7 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-7">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="604.70857142857" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e7/Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"' data-title="Original Ogg file (60 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="59718"></source><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e7/Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga/Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="116032"></source></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wal-Mart_Workers_and_Wal-Mart-Free_NYC_at_Occupy_Wall_Street.vorb.oga" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers speak during <a href="/wiki/Occupy_Wall_Street" title="Occupy Wall Street">Occupy Wall Street</a></div></div></div>
<p>With over 2.3 million employees worldwide, Walmart has faced a torrent of lawsuits and issues with regards to its workforce. These issues involve <a href="/wiki/Wage" title="Wage">low wages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Occupational_safety_and_health" title="Occupational safety and health">poor working conditions</a>, inadequate <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">health care</a>, and issues involving the company's strong <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">anti-union</a> policies. In November 2013, the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Board" title="National Labor Relations Board">National Labor Relations Board</a> (NLRB) announced that it had found that in 13<span class="nowrap"> </span>U.S. states, Wal-Mart had pressured employees not to engage in strikes on Black Friday, and had illegally disciplined workers who had engaged in strikes.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup> Critics point to Walmart's high <a href="/wiki/Turnover_(employment)" title="Turnover (employment)">turnover</a> rate as evidence of an unhappy workforce, although other factors may be involved. Approximately 70<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of its employees leave within the first year.<sup id="cite_ref-Store_Wars_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Store_Wars-350">[350]</a></sup> Despite this turnover rate, the company is still able to affect unemployment rates. This was found in a study by Oklahoma State University which states, "Walmart is found to have substantially lowered the relative unemployment rates of blacks in those counties where it is present, but to have had only a limited impact on relative incomes after the influences of other socio-economic variables were taken into account."<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup></p><p>Walmart is the largest private employer in the United States, employing almost five times as many people as <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, the second-largest employer.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">[352]</a></sup> Walmart employs more <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> than any other private employer in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT4115_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT4115-353">[353]</a></sup></p><p>Walmart rebranded their Associate Education Benefits to Live Better U in March 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-news.walmart.com_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.walmart.com-354">[354]</a></sup> Live Better U supports associate education at every level and includes $1 a day college program, cost-free high school education, and discounts on higher education programs through partnership with Guild Education.<sup id="cite_ref-news.walmart.com_354-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.walmart.com-354">[354]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2019 Walmart Inc. announced plans to extend the use of robots in stores in order to improve  and monitor <a href="/wiki/Inventory" title="Inventory">inventory</a>, clean floors and unload trucks, part of the company's effort to lower its labor costs.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup></p><p>In June 2019, Walmart Inc. announced the expansion of education benefits to recruit high school students. The incentives include flexible work schedules, free SAT and ACT preparation courses, up to seven hours of free college credit, and a debt-free college degree in three fields from six nonprofit universities.<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">[356]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Gender" data-section="49" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 2007, a <a href="/wiki/Discrimination" title="Discrimination">gender discrimination</a> lawsuit, <i><a href="/wiki/Dukes_v._Wal-Mart_Stores,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Dukes v. Wal-Mart Stores, Inc.">Dukes v. Wal-Mart Stores, Inc.</a></i>, was filed against Walmart, alleging that female employees were discriminated against in matters regarding pay and promotions. A <a href="/wiki/Class_action" title="Class action">class action</a> suit was sought, which would have been the nation's largest in history, covering 1.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>million past and current employees.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenhouse_07_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhouse_07-357">[357]</a></sup> On June 20, 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">United States Supreme Court</a> ruled in Wal-Mart's favor, stating that the plaintiffs did not have enough in common to constitute a class.<sup id="cite_ref-SCOTUS_Walmart_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCOTUS_Walmart-358">[358]</a></sup> The court ruled unanimously that because of the variability of the plaintiffs' circumstances, the class action could not proceed as presented, and furthermore, in a 5–4 decision that it could not proceed as any kind of class action suit.<sup id="cite_ref-Lennard_Natasha_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lennard_Natasha-359">[359]</a></sup> Several plaintiffs, including the lead plaintiff, Betty Dukes, expressed their intent to file individual discrimination lawsuits separately.<sup id="cite_ref-Clifford_Stephanie_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clifford_Stephanie-360">[360]</a></sup></p><p>According to a consultant hired by plaintiffs in a sex discrimination lawsuit, in 2001, Wal-Mart's <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">Equal Employment Opportunity Commission</a> filings showed that female employees made up 65 percent of Wal-Mart's hourly paid workforce, but only 33<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of its management.<sup id="cite_ref-Conlin_01_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conlin_01-361">[361]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zellner_362-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-362">[362]</a></sup> Just 35<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of its store managers were women, compared to 57 percent at similar retailers.<sup id="cite_ref-zellner_362-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-362">[362]</a></sup> Wal-Mart says comparisons with other retailers are unfair, because it classifies employees differently; if department managers were included in the totals, women would make up 60<span class="nowrap"> </span>percent of the managerial ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-zellner_362-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zellner-362">[362]</a></sup> Others have criticized the lawsuit as without basis in the law and as an abuse of the class action mechanism.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">[363]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">[364]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">[365]</a></sup> In 2007, Wal-Mart was named by the National Association for Female Executives as one of the top<span class="nowrap"> </span>35 companies for executive women.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">[366]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_orientation_and_gender_identity">Sexual orientation and gender identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Sexual orientation and gender identity" data-section="50" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Campaign" title="Human Rights Campaign">Human Rights Campaign</a>'s (HRC) 2002 <a href="/wiki/Corporate_Equality_Index" title="Corporate Equality Index">Corporate Equality Index</a>, a measure of how companies treat <a href="/wiki/LGBT" title="LGBT">LGBT</a> employees and customers, gave Wal-Mart Stores Inc. a score of 14%.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">[367]</a></sup> By 2017, however, HRC's 2017 Corporate Equality Index gave Wal-Mart Stores Inc. a score of a 100%.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">[368]</a></sup> In 2003, Walmart added sexual orientation to their anti-discrimination policy.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">[369]</a></sup> In 2005, Walmart's definition of family began including <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex partners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">[370]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">[371]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EqualityIndex16_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EqualityIndex16-372">[372]</a></sup> In 2006, Walmart announced that "diversity efforts include new groups of minority, female and <a href="/wiki/Gay" title="Gay">gay</a> employees that meet at Walmart headquarters in Bentonville to advise the company on marketing and internal promotion. There are seven business resource groups: women, <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native Americans</a>, gays and <a href="/wiki/Lesbian" title="Lesbian">lesbians</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disabled</a> group."<sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">[373]</a></sup> From 2006 to 2008, Walmart was a member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Gay_%26_Lesbian_Chamber_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="National Gay &amp; Lesbian Chamber of Commerce">National Gay &amp; Lesbian Chamber of Commerce</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walmart's_remarkable_gay_rights_journey_374-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walmart's_remarkable_gay_rights_journey-374">[374]</a></sup> In 2011, Walmart added <a href="/wiki/Gender_identity" title="Gender identity">gender identity</a> to their anti-discrimination policy.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">[375]</a></sup> Walmart's anti-discrimination policies allow associates to use restroom facilities that corresponds with their gender identity and <a href="/wiki/Gender_expression" title="Gender expression">gender expression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">[376]</a></sup> In 2013, Walmart began offering <a href="/wiki/Health_insurance" title="Health insurance">health insurance</a> benefits to <a href="/wiki/Domestic_partnership" title="Domestic partnership">domestic partners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Walmart's_remarkable_gay_rights_journey_374-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walmart's_remarkable_gay_rights_journey-374">[374]</a></sup> In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Doug_McMillon" title="Doug McMillon">Doug McMillon</a>, CEO of Walmart, issued a statement opposing <a href="/wiki/Arkansas_HB_1228" title="Arkansas HB 1228">House Bill 1228</a> and asked Governor <a href="/wiki/Asa_Hutchinson" title="Asa Hutchinson">Asa Hutchinson</a> to veto the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">[377]</a></sup> In 2016, Walmart added full healthcare benefits to its transgender employees.<sup id="cite_ref-378" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-378">[378]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(8)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_and_controversies">Criticism and controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Criticism and controversies" data-section="51" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-8 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-8">
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Walmart" title="Criticism of Walmart">Criticism of Walmart</a></div>
<p>Walmart has been subject to criticism from various groups and individuals, including <a href="/wiki/Labor_union" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor union">labor unions</a>, community groups, <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">grassroots</a> organizations, religious organizations, environmental groups, firearm groups, and the company's own customers and employees. They have protested against the company's policies and business practices, including charges of racial and gender discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-mkabel_379-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mkabel-379">[379]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-380" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-380">[380]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-381" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-381">[381]</a></sup> Other areas of criticism include the company's foreign product sourcing, treatment of suppliers, employee compensation and working conditions, environmental practices, <a href="/wiki/Corporate_welfare" title="Corporate welfare">the use of public subsidies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labor_spies#Wal-Mart_surveillance_of_employees" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor spies">the company's security policies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-382" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-382">[382]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-383" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-383">[383]</a></sup> Walmart denies doing anything wrong and maintains that low prices are the result of efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-384" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-384">[384]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-385" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-385">[385]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-386" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-386">[386]</a></sup></p><p>In April 2016, Walmart announced that it plans to eliminate eggs from <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cages</a> from its supply chain by 2025.<sup id="cite_ref-387" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-387">[387]</a></sup> The decision was particularly important because of Walmart's large <a href="/wiki/Market_share" title="Market share">market share</a> and influence on the rest of the industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FortuneEgg_388-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FortuneEgg-388">[388]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BloombergEgg_389-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BloombergEgg-389">[389]</a></sup> The move was praised by major <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> groups<sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_390-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-390">[390]</a></sup> but a poultry trade group representative expressed skepticism about the decision's impact.<sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_390-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-390">[390]</a></sup> Walmart's cage-free eggs will not come from <a href="/wiki/Free_range" title="Free range">free range</a> producers, but rather <a href="/wiki/Factory_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Factory farming">industrial-scale farms</a> where the birds will be allotted between 1 and 1.5 square feet each, a stressful arrangement which can cause <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism_in_poultry" title="Cannibalism in poultry">cannibalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FortuneEgg_388-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FortuneEgg-388">[388]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Modern_Farmer_390-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Modern_Farmer-390">[390]</a></sup> Unlike battery cages, the systems Walmart's suppliers will allow the hens to move around, but relative to battery cages they have higher hen mortality rates and present distinct environmental and worker health problems.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_Gelles_391-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT_Gelles-391">[391]</a></sup></p><p>In March 2018, Walmart was sued by former Director of Business Development Tri Huynh for claims of reporting misleading e-commerce performance results in favor of the company. Huynh stated the company's move was an attempt to regain lost ground to competitor <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-392" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-392">[392]</a></sup></p><p>In September 2018, Walmart was sued by <a href="/wiki/Equal_Employment_Opportunity_Commission" title="Equal Employment Opportunity Commission">Equal Employment Opportunity Commission</a> alleging that Walmart denied requests from pregnant employees to limit heavy lifting.<sup id="cite_ref-393" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-393">[393]</a></sup></p><p>In May 2019, the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Inquiry" title="Center for Inquiry">Center for Inquiry</a> filed a lawsuit in the District of Columbia alleging consumer fraud and the endangering of its customers' health due to Walmart's practice of "selling <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathic [products]</a> alongside real medicine, in the same sections in its stores, under the same signs," according to Nicholas Little, CFI's vice president and general counsel.<sup id="cite_ref-394" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-394">[394]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-395" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-395">[395]</a></sup></p><p>In July 2019, the Walmart <a href="/wiki/Subreddit" class="mw-redirect" title="Subreddit">subreddit</a> was flooded with pro-union memes in a protest to the firing of an employee who posted confidential material to the subreddit.<sup id="cite_ref-396" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-396">[396]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-397" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-397">[397]</a></sup> Many of these posts were angry with Walmart surveying its staff on the Internet. The posting of the union content is in response to the aforementioned alleged anti-union position Walmart has taken in the past.<sup id="cite_ref-398" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-398">[398]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="2010s_crime_problem">2010s crime problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: 2010s crime problem" data-section="52" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>According to an August 2016 report by <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg Businessweek</a></i>, aggressive cost-cutting decisions that began in 2000 when Lee Scott took over as CEO of the company led to a significant increase in crime in stores across the United States. These included the removal of the store's famed greeters, which are in part seen as a theft deterrent at exits, the replacement of many cashiers with self-checkout stations, and the addition of stores at a rate that exceeded the hiring of new employees which led to a 19% increase in space per employee from a decade previous. While these decisions succeeded in increasing profits 23% in the decade that followed, they also led to an increase in both theft and violent crime.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_399-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-399">[399]</a></sup></p><p>In 2015, under CEO <a href="/wiki/Doug_McMillon" title="Doug McMillon">Doug McMillon</a>, Walmart began a company-wide campaign to reduce crime that included spot-checking receipts at exits, stationing employees at self-checkout areas, eye-level security cameras in high-theft areas, use of data analytics to detect credit fraud, hiring off-duty police and private security officers, and reducing calls to police with a program by which first-time offenders caught stealing merchandise below a certain value can avoid arrest if they agree to go through a theft-prevention program.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_399-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-399">[399]</a></sup></p><p>However, some law enforcement professionals have said the improvements are coming too slowly.  A longtime police veteran in Tulsa, Oklahoma, stated, "It’s ridiculous—we are talking about the biggest retailer in the world.  I may have half my squad there for hours."  A police captain in Port Richey, Florida, stated, "They recognize the problem and refuse to do anything about it."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_399-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-399">[399]</a></sup></p><p>Law enforcement agencies across the United States have noted a burden on resources created by a disproportionate number of calls from Walmart. Experts have criticized the retailer for shifting its security burden onto the taxpayers. They found that 25% of arrests overall in St. Petersburg, Florida are made at just one Walmart. Across three Florida counties, approximately 9,000 police calls were logged to 53 Walmart stores but resulted in only a few hundred arrests.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_400-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-400">[400]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Granite_Falls,_North_Carolina" title="Granite Falls, North Carolina">Granite Falls, North Carolina</a>, 92% of <a href="/wiki/Larceny" title="Larceny">larceny</a> calls to local police were from the Walmart store there.<sup id="cite_ref-401" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-401">[401]</a></sup> The trend is similar in rural, suburban, and urban areas. Police are called to Walmart stores 3 to 4 times as much as similar retailers such as Target.<sup id="cite_ref-402" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-402">[402]</a></sup> Experts say the chain and its razor-thin profit margins rely heavily on police to protect its bottom line. Walmart Supercenters top the list of those most visited by police.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_400-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-400">[400]</a></sup></p><p>The police captain in Port Richey, Florida, said that Target stores more often have uniformed security, as well as more visible staff in any case.  Another comparison might be shopping malls which often have security patrols and off-duty police officers. J.R. Roberts, a former director for risk management at Valor Security Services (which provides mall security) says: "Shopping centers all have security; they know it’s an expense, but one they know pays dividends because people feel safer going to their stores."<sup id="cite_ref-:1_399-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-399">[399]</a></sup></p><p>In addition to hundreds of thousands of petty crimes, more than 200 violent crimes, including attempted kidnappings, stabbings, shootings, and murders occurred at the 4,500 Walmarts in the U.S. in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_399-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-399">[399]</a></sup> In 2019, 22 people were killed in a <a href="/wiki/2019_El_Paso_shooting" title="2019 El Paso shooting">mass shooting at a Walmart store in El Paso, Texas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Curbs_317-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curbs-317">[317]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(9)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Walmart&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: See also" data-section="53" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-9 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-9">

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">April 13,</span> 2014</span>. <q>Wal-Mart Stores has seen the light.
